<div id="review_61940857" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/61940857" />
    <a title="Amy Reed" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1717983-amy-reed"><img alt="Amy Reed" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442499031p2/1717983.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/61940857?book_show_action=true">Jul 02, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Amy Reed" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Amy Reed" href="/user/show/1717983-amy-reed">Amy Reed</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">smart people</span></div>

        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer61940857" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8823276233509250991">OK, I have officially given up on this book. I have 75 pages left, but I just can't do it. For the last few days, I have picked it up only to put it down again as soon as I read the first sentence five times. I feel stupid. This guy is a Nobel Prize winner, so that must mean I'm an idiot if I don't like him, right? <br/><br/>This book is smart, don't get me wrong. It's obvious a genius wrote it. But I don't read for intellectual stimulation unless I'm in a literature class. Perhaps that makes me a lazy re</span>
  <span id="freeText8823276233509250991" style="display:none">OK, I have officially given up on this book. I have 75 pages left, but I just can't do it. For the last few days, I have picked it up only to put it down again as soon as I read the first sentence five times. I feel stupid. This guy is a Nobel Prize winner, so that must mean I'm an idiot if I don't like him, right? <br /><br />This book is smart, don't get me wrong. It's obvious a genius wrote it. But I don't read for intellectual stimulation unless I'm in a literature class. Perhaps that makes me a lazy reader. If I'm going to spend my little free time with a book, I need it to move me emotionally. That's just the way I roll. I feel like I should be ashamed of this. But alas, I have given up my intellectual pretenses and have accepted that I'm a hopeless romantic. I need a book to make me cry if I'm going to like it. This book made me think "ooh, isn't this author smart" but I felt nothing for the characters. And that's saying a lot because the main character is Jesus Christ, who you'd think would be a pretty dynamic character.<br /><br />I'd love to know what other people think about this book if they've read it. </span>
  <a data-text-id="8823276233509250991" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Jareed" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/17117710-jareed"><img alt="Jareed" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1399195347p2/17117710.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/816639867?book_show_action=true">Jan 08, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jareed" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jareed" href="/user/show/17117710-jareed">Jareed</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">1</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/17117710-jareed?shelf=fictional-novels">fictional-novels</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/17117710-jareed?shelf=written-by-nobel-laureates">written-by-nobel-laureates</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer816639867" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11726723572901448611">Also posted on my blog:<a href='http://imbookedindefinitely.blogspot.com/2014/05/the-gospel-according-to-jesus-christ-by.html' rel='nofollow noopener'>i'mbookedindefinitely</a><br/><br/>Appreciation of this book dictates that you have to contend with two premises:<br/><br/><b>First:</b> That Saramago's signature writing is characterized by sentences that are paragraphs long occasionally digressing from the thought of the sentence. <br/><br/><b>Second:</b> That this book is about the humanization of Jesus Christ necessarily entailing innumerable repercussions to the orthodox belief of his 'socially' constructed divinity.<br/><br/>Of the two, I met with some negligible difficulty</span>
  <span id="freeText11726723572901448611" style="display:none">Also posted on my blog:<a href="http://imbookedindefinitely.blogspot.com/2014/05/the-gospel-according-to-jesus-christ-by.html" rel="nofollow noopener">i'mbookedindefinitely</a><br /><br />Appreciation of this book dictates that you have to contend with two premises:<br /><br /><b>First:</b> That Saramago's signature writing is characterized by sentences that are paragraphs long occasionally digressing from the thought of the sentence. <br /><br /><b>Second:</b> That this book is about the humanization of Jesus Christ necessarily entailing innumerable repercussions to the orthodox belief of his 'socially' constructed divinity.<br /><br />Of the two, I met with some negligible difficulty with former. <br /><br />Saramago's writing is incomparably unique and beautiful at the same time. It is unique for his sentences are literally paragraphs long lengthened by serial commas and seemingly unending semicolons. But Saramago's writing does not lack beauty. In fact even in his long winded sentences, there is fluidity in his labyrinthine like thoughts. It cannot be hardly expected that sentences as long as his comport themselves into solitary thoughts. This is hardly the case as his sentences sometimes tend to digress from the precedents he has laid down. I often find myself pausing irregularly, suddenly turning pages back, and rereading certain passages over again. But once you find the coherence in his thought and the connection between the phrases, one will find writing unlike any other. <br /><br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/28859.The_Gospel_According_to_Jesus_Christ" title="The Gospel According to Jesus Christ by Jos√© Saramago" rel="nofollow noopener">The Gospel According to Jesus Christ</a> is an incomparably fearless work. Works like these tend to create an unprecedented outcry from the religious community, as it did in this case, which however did not impede Saramago winning the Nobel Prize for Literature. <br /><br />In most Christian orthodox beliefs, the arrangement of the written Word, what books to include and not, were contemplated over a time span of centuries and most were in pursuit of a certain desired conclusion, the rendering of Jesus Christ‚Äôs divinity. Also, Jesus formative years from 12 years of age to 30 are not recorded or written. This book is a fictional retelling of that life, including the years before his ministry. <br /><br />This is an attempt on the humanization of the life of Jesus Christ. He is portrayed as flawed, easily angered, subjected to passions, desires and doubts; he is portrayed as <i>
  <b>‚Äúhuman‚Äù</b>
</i>. As an extract, he wrote how Jesus fell in love with Magdalene, learning the warmth and passion of the loins and how through his ministry, she remained to be on her side, as an apostle no less. <br /><br />The controversy over these kinds of themes and books has always been curious to me. The contention that lies on the alleged challenges to his divinity is misplaced and unnecessary. I guess people tend to forget the fact that Jesus was born of a human mother, but more importantly, he himself was both burdened and liberated by the human flesh. He was subject to pain, hunger, and passion naturally. We have here a perfect human model who was able to rise above the yoke of the world, but in failing to acknowledge that he himself was condemned to the very maladies we are subject to, we tend to be caught in a cyclic self-defeating belief. You see, faith in Jesus Christ should not be that he was faultless, kind, and magnanimous because he was divine. It should be anchored on the idea that he was human, like us, and that he saw kindness in the world, he gave compassion where none was asked, he made the right and honest decisions were conventionality dictated otherwise. <br /><br />To consider the question of this book as an affront to the faithful is both hilarious and tautological. Faith is a funny thing. It is meant to be tested, to weather it from the storms of challenges, to keep resolute in times of trials and temptations, to be unyielding in the most pressing of times, there lies faith, not in the cradled bosoms of the fearful and the apprehensive. <br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br />For a similar book to enjoy, read <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/912892307?book_show_action=false" rel="nofollow noopener">Revolt of the Angels by Anatole France (4 Stars)</a><br /><br /><br />This book forms part of my remarkably extensive reading list on <a href="http://imbookedindefinitely.blogspot.com/p/nobel-prize-for-literature.html" rel="nofollow noopener">Nobel Prize for Literature Awardees</a><br /></span>

    <a title="sologdin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5610133-sologdin"><img alt="sologdin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1307928658p2/5610133.jpg" /></a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="sologdin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="sologdin" href="/user/show/5610133-sologdin">sologdin</a>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5610133-sologdin?shelf=of-best-sentence-and-moost-solaas">of-best-sentence-and-moost-solaas</a>,

              <span id="reviewTextContainer870098467" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer14605024254385130567">Early on, ‚ÄúMary‚Äôs legs were now open, perhaps they had opened by themselves as she dreamed and she did not close them out of this sudden lassitude, or else from the premonition of a married woman who knows her duty [‚Ä¶] the holy seed of Joseph poured into the holy womb of Mary‚Äù (13), sufficiently heretical without the immediate afterthought: ‚Äúthere are things God Himself does not understand‚Äù (id.). Don‚Äôt worry--this is merely prelude to Jesus & Magdalena: ‚ÄúDiscover your body, and there it was, te</span>
  <span id="freeText14605024254385130567" style="display:none">Early on, ‚ÄúMary‚Äôs legs were now open, perhaps they had opened by themselves as she dreamed and she did not close them out of this sudden lassitude, or else from the premonition of a married woman who knows her duty [‚Ä¶] the holy seed of Joseph poured into the holy womb of Mary‚Äù (13), sufficiently heretical without the immediate afterthought: ‚Äúthere are things God Himself does not understand‚Äù (id.). Don‚Äôt worry--this is merely prelude to Jesus &amp; Magdalena: ‚ÄúDiscover your body, and there it was, tense, taut, roused, and she, naked and magnificent, was above him and saying, There is nothing fear, do not move, leave this to me. Then he felt part of him, this organ, disappearing inside her, a ring of fire around it, coming and going, a shudder passed through him, like a wriggling fish slipping free with a shout, surely impossible, fish do not shout, no, it was he, Jesus, crying out as Mary slumped over his body with a moan‚Äù (237)--which is something of a miracle, as JC should‚Äôve been like the 40-Year Old Virgin here instead of Ron Jeremy.<br /><br />So, yeah, it‚Äôs that kind of book. Jesus is born in a cave (58). Some dicking around in the beginning about who actually knocked Mary up--Satan, YHWH, big Joe.<br /><br />Running commentary throughout on gender politics, such as ‚ÄúMary is neither upright nor pious, but she is not to blame for this, the blame lies with the language she speaks if not with the men who invented it, because that language has no feminine form for the words upright and pious‚Äù (16) or ‚Äúwe mustn‚Äôt forget that this whole process, from the moment of impregnation to the moment of birth, is unclean, that vile female organ, vortex and abyss, the seat of all the world‚Äôs evil, an inner labyrinth of blood, discharges, gushing water, revolting afterbirth, dear God‚Äù (54). <br /><br />We get a <i>Life of Brian</i> joke: ‚ÄúThe is not the only couple called Joseph and Mary expecting a baby, who perhaps two infants of the same sex, preferably male, will be born at the same hour and only a road or a field of corn between them. The destinies that await these infants, however, will be different‚Äù (52). <br /><br />In addition to gentle political ribbing and express heresy, there‚Äôs effective satire throughout, such as the alphabetical listing of martyrs (321-25) and the history of Jesus‚Äô foreskin (63) (I love the latter particularly because my own favorite internet alt 15 years ago was Christ‚Äôs Foreskin; it was the ideal prophylactic to slip on and penetrate online religious discussions.)<br /><br />Basic engine of the narrative is that Joe overhears plans for Herod‚Äôs slaughter of infants and runs back to save Jesus, but doesn‚Äôt bother to warn anyone else, and thereafter carries guilt for this omission; novel intones ‚ÄúA father‚Äôs guilt falls on the heads of his children‚Äù (88), and so Christ carries this with him after Joe gets crucified by the bloody Romans (it really is very difficult to read this without thinking Monty Python.) There‚Äôs a great moment where Jesus literally steps into Joe‚Äôs shoes as a subrogee (137). Christ is haunted by dream visions of the slaughter of innocents, and author reminds us that ‚Äúit may seem inappropriate to put the complex theories of modern thinkers into the head of a Palestinian who lived so many years before Freud, Jung, Groddeck, and Lacan‚Äù (162).<br /><br />Anyway, ‚ÄúGod does not forgive the sins He makes us commit‚Äù (127). And ‚Äúman is free [‚Ä¶] so that he might be punished‚Äù (171). But cf.: ‚Äúyour God is the only warden of a prison where the only prisoner is your God‚Äù (197). It is ‚Äúimpossible to tell the difference between an angel of the Lord and an angel of Satan‚Äù (213). (The Lucifer character is very interesting--not Milton-interesting, but alright.)<br /><br />Overall, zips right along. <br /><br />Recommended for those who learn that certain things can be understood only if we take the trouble to trace them to their origins, incubuses who violate women in their sleep, and readers who show that supreme indifference which we associate with the universe, or that other absolute indifference, the indifference of emptiness, which will remain, if there is such a thing as emptiness, when all has been fulfilled.<br /></span>

<div id="review_63635213" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/63635213" />
    <a title="Jason" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2526106-jason"><img alt="Jason" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1557331096p2/2526106.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/63635213?book_show_action=true">Jul 15, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jason" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jason" href="/user/show/2526106-jason">Jason</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780156001410" href="/book/show/2527.The_Gospel_According_to_Jesus_Christ">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2526106-jason?shelf=translated-into-english">translated-into-english</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2526106-jason?shelf=nobel-prize">nobel-prize</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2526106-jason?shelf=christianity">christianity</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2526106-jason?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2526106-jason?shelf=religion">religion</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer63635213" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14157362235255501588">In this novel Saramago has created a masterpiece, both in terms of style and content.<br/><br/>The prose is musical, poetically evocative of the streams of consciousness and conversation that fill our lives. While Saramago's paragraphs often run several pages in length, and his idiosyncratic use of punctuation (e.g., his refusal to use quotation marks to delimit speech and his insistence on ending all sentences--including questions--with a period) can seem daunting, the fluid, melodic language makes readi</span>
  <span id="freeText14157362235255501588" style="display:none">In this novel Saramago has created a masterpiece, both in terms of style and content.<br /><br />The prose is musical, poetically evocative of the streams of consciousness and conversation that fill our lives. While Saramago's paragraphs often run several pages in length, and his idiosyncratic use of punctuation (e.g., his refusal to use quotation marks to delimit speech and his insistence on ending all sentences--including questions--with a period) can seem daunting, the fluid, melodic language makes reading the story a true pleasure.<br /><br />In terms of content, Saramago has mastered the art of faithfully retelling a story while simultaneously subverting the text through interesting asides, editorial comments, notes to the reader, etc. He also subverts the normal relationship between humanity and God in Christian tradition, wherein humanity is in need of God's forgiveness. In Saramago's retelling, it is the inhumanity of God that is need of humanity's forgiveness. I have often wondered at the needless brutality that lies at the core of mainstream Christian theology (i.e., God needing to have his only child brutally murdered in order to forgive me for being the imperfect being I was created to be), and finally, in Saramago, I have found an author willing to take God to task while not dismissing the sorrowful beauty of the life of Jesus.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14157362235255501588" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_23695706" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/23695706" />
    <a title="Michael" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/348666-michael"><img alt="Michael" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1346855114p2/348666.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/23695706?book_show_action=true">Jun 04, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Michael" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Michael" href="/user/show/348666-michael">Michael</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780156001410" href="/book/show/2527.The_Gospel_According_to_Jesus_Christ">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer23695706" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13508332620482793047">Let me say at the outset that this book will probably be quite a challenge to anyone who holds (and doesn‚Äôt want to let go) a very traditional, orthodox reading of the traditional gospels in the Christian Testament. What Saramago has done here is to IMAGINE a gospel that places at least equal weight on Jesus as divine AND human. He also IMAGINES God (the Father, that is) who might also be both divine and human (as the ancient Greek gods were, with all their jealousies, rivalries, self-interests,</span>
  <span id="freeText13508332620482793047" style="display:none">Let me say at the outset that this book will probably be quite a challenge to anyone who holds (and doesn‚Äôt want to let go) a very traditional, orthodox reading of the traditional gospels in the Christian Testament. What Saramago has done here is to IMAGINE a gospel that places at least equal weight on Jesus as divine AND human. He also IMAGINES God (the Father, that is) who might also be both divine and human (as the ancient Greek gods were, with all their jealousies, rivalries, self-interests, etc). At times we see Jesus being pushed and pulled by a god (little ‚Äúg‚Äù) very different from what we‚Äôre use to. Just remember, this is not a re-telling of the traditional story, but an imaginative work of art. I would also venture to say that Saramago has told a universal story. At the risk of sounding like a ‚Äúnew age‚Äù guru, aren‚Äôt all humans both divine and human? I have always loved the ancient gods. They always seemed so real to me. Saramago, I think, succeeds in telling a story that is about all of us. I can't decide if I like this story better that "Blindness." For the time being, I'd place them on equal status.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13508332620482793047" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  <div class="left bodycol">

        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1064964311?book_show_action=true">Sep 26, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="David" class="user" itemprop="url" name="David" href="/user/show/8311987-david">David</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1064964311" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15943007476394313992">Let's face it, I'm infatuated by the writing of Jos√© Saramago, and will continue to read the few novels he wrote that I have not yet had the chance to. Granted, his style takes some getting used to, with the paragraph long sentences, liberal use of commas, and complete lack of conventional standards for dialogue.<br/><br/>The book is certainly blasphemous from a purely theological standpoint, as Saramago was an atheist, but make no mistake; this is a towering work of literature. The characters are fleshed</span>
  <span id="freeText15943007476394313992" style="display:none">Let's face it, I'm infatuated by the writing of Jos√© Saramago, and will continue to read the few novels he wrote that I have not yet had the chance to. Granted, his style takes some getting used to, with the paragraph long sentences, liberal use of commas, and complete lack of conventional standards for dialogue.<br /><br />The book is certainly blasphemous from a purely theological standpoint, as Saramago was an atheist, but make no mistake; this is a towering work of literature. The characters are fleshed out so beautifully, their joys and sorrows detailed so carefully; their lives brought to life through the written word. Saramago's prose is very hard to top in its haunting beauty; I must have quoted at least two to three dozen passages.<br /><br />This humanistic perspective of Jesus, along with the portrayal of Mary Magdalene, the apostles, particularly the infamous Judas Iscariot, as well as God and 'Pastor' were absolutely fantastic, and wove a wonderful, if not horrifying tale.<br /><br />I can't say enough about Saramago's genius. One of my favorite 'contemporary' authors. <br /><br />Highest possible rating!<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="15943007476394313992" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/447914666?book_show_action=true">Nov 03, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Shaimaa Ali" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Shaimaa Ali" href="/user/show/4176602-shaimaa-ali">Shaimaa Ali</a>

          added it


          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780099466847" href="/book/show/11005533-the-gospel-according-to-jesus-christ">review of another edition</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer447914666" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7046385033852126406">One of the rarest times ever that i'm not able to rate a book!<br/>Saramago at his best & worst at the same time!!<br/><br/>I've read lots of Saramago novels (Blindness , Seeing, The year of the Death of Ricardo Reis, the Cave, Manual of Painting and Calligraphy, the voyage of the elephant) and still have others by him to read.. My luck lead me today to start reading "The Gospel" as it has a very seductive title.. <br/>Knowing that Saramago was an atheist before start reading this novel will make it a bit of a reli</span>
  <span id="freeText7046385033852126406" style="display:none">One of the rarest times ever that i'm not able to rate a book!<br />Saramago at his best &amp; worst at the same time!!<br /><br />I've read lots of Saramago novels (Blindness , Seeing, The year of the Death of Ricardo Reis, the Cave, Manual of Painting and Calligraphy, the voyage of the elephant) and still have others by him to read.. My luck lead me today to start reading "The Gospel" as it has a very seductive title.. <br />Knowing that Saramago was an atheist before start reading this novel will make it a bit of a relief as the shocks inside it are numerous!! The Gospel here is against my Islamic &amp; even Christians' believes .. However you can't deny how genius he was in illustrating the life of The Christ as he imagined it, sound strange right? But the deep conversations , questions raised between Jesus , God and the Devil are amazing .. Eye opening ! You are not to think later of how easy it is to defeat atheists questions and defend your religion.. It's never easy!! Saramago is not denying the existence of God ..if he did he won't come with all those painful questions .. Won't write four whole pages with the names of the Christian Martyrs through the years in alphabetical order! .. Won't even mention Islam with the deep voice coming from the mist questioning if they are probably the same God or not!!<br /><br />Saramago was cynical , provoking and humorous at the same time, i started by cursing him thousand times at the start of the novel as he kept on twisting the truth , but from literature point of view he was brilliant!<br />Not recommended for any religious reader, it will rage you to the bones, but will enlighten you if you chose to use such claims against God to strengthen your faith !! </span>
  <a data-text-id="7046385033852126406" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_260689682" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/260689682" />
    <a title="Leonor R. Fernandes" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6634963-leonor-r-fernandes"><img alt="Leonor R. Fernandes" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1596898740p2/6634963.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/260689682?book_show_action=true">Jan 12, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Leonor R. Fernandes" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Leonor R. Fernandes" href="/user/show/6634963-leonor-r-fernandes">Leonor R. Fernandes</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/1103769.O_Evangelho_Segundo_Jesus_Cristo">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer260689682" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10449354101783281379">What is great literature but to absolutely despise so many things about a book overall, character development, plot construction, and even so living in the constant realization that in front of your eyes lies the work of a genius? I started this book the way I start every book written by Saramago; expecting to see hatred, bitterness, the wrong amount of pretentious, and every time I come back to his works for sheer amusement. <br/>Above all, the truth is that Saramago does to the reader what any read</span>
  <span id="freeText10449354101783281379" style="display:none">What is great literature but to absolutely despise so many things about a book overall, character development, plot construction, and even so living in the constant realization that in front of your eyes lies the work of a genius? I started this book the way I start every book written by Saramago; expecting to see hatred, bitterness, the wrong amount of pretentious, and every time I come back to his works for sheer amusement. <br />Above all, the truth is that Saramago does to the reader what any reader intends to have done to them ‚Äì a way to make us the one who writes, or, if we prefer, the one who narrates, the one who knows every step of every character and how a story unwinds within the story; a challenge, to accept that the story can be what we make of it, to accept that the story can be something completely different from what we set it out to be before, that the world and life itself are, in such a way, unforeseen, that the devil can become a Shepherd and that Jesus can, after all, be a wrongdoer, that literature can able us to live fiction as it was reality, for during those 500 pages, that is our reality; Saramago does to the reader, that which a good writer cannot do, that which, inevitably, only the best writers can do: knowing a work as fiction, and even then perceive it as wholly beautiful, and feel it, most of the times, has harshly real ‚Äì even though we know it to be unreal, or unknown.<br />The genius in Saramago, and maybe that is the reason we can use this word, is transversal to everything a work can contain; it‚Äôs not only about a well-built plot, but also a linguistic mastering that takes our breath away and makes us want to keep going, even with shortness of breath, until the very last page. As if we cannot allow the reading to cease at the first full stop, as if at every comma a new stimulus sprouts into existence, a new urgency in sucking everything the author has to tell us. If any literary geniuses exist, or have ever existed, we cannot afford not to mention Jos√© Saramago as one of them. I say this as an incredulous reader that with every book from this writer gets more surprised, not only for it not being, after all, a bore, but for being, unquestionably, always a good surprise, a good place to come back to. Uncomfortable, also, but beautiful, precisely for it.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10449354101783281379" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  <span id="freeText14686043832558963005" style="display:none">What is the whole point behind reading a book about Jesus Christ? Isn‚Äôt is to get inspired and be filled with hope? If I want to get philosophical, I am not going to read about Jesus Christ but will look in Aristotle‚Äôs works for instance. Saramago wants to tell us that God doesn‚Äôt care and it is all up to us. This is wrong: God does care about every single creature of his whether they believe in him or not. His divine Providence is always amazingly at work. I will give the book credit for its ferocious wit, yes wit indeed that your brain has to work in full gear. There is poetry in the book, but it is his own Portuguese kind of poetry. As a person like me thinking in English, I would rather seek the English sense of poetry, and when I am reading in Portuguese I am going to be after it in its own spirit, not translated, because some things don‚Äôt translate easily into other languages, and I know that form firsthand experience in reading classical Arabic poetry translated into English- it is not the same. </span>

          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780156001410" href="/book/show/2527.The_Gospel_According_to_Jesus_Christ">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7405895-shari?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7405895-shari?shelf=award-winner">award-winner</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer277168875" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17602633112264917287">Thought-provoking. This book will anger, provoke, inspire and bewilder its readers. For the religious, and Bible fanatics, this book requires an open mind to be tackled, because in it the life and passion of Christ are presented in a different light. The major elements of the four New Testament gospels about the life of Christ are mostly there, but Saramago not only mixed them around, but added and omitted some as well. Jesus in this novel is filled with questions and doubts. For him, God's acti</span>
  <span id="freeText17602633112264917287" style="display:none">Thought-provoking. This book will anger, provoke, inspire and bewilder its readers. For the religious, and Bible fanatics, this book requires an open mind to be tackled, because in it the life and passion of Christ are presented in a different light. The major elements of the four New Testament gospels about the life of Christ are mostly there, but Saramago not only mixed them around, but added and omitted some as well. Jesus in this novel is filled with questions and doubts. For him, God's actions are not absolute. He obeys God's bidding to be sacrificed not because he believes, but because he has no choice. He spiritually grumbles as he does them, not because he is a "bad" son, but because he thinks there is a better way to make people believe. In Saramago's world, God is equally responsible for the evil that is happening around. </span>
  <a data-text-id="17602633112264917287" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('RIrc92OUhVr3Z37OQQg3hj/17lHB3qmfuVDahDjDkMF+rLUl2MpMMlBMyq+2/T1Z6aoJmQ9TJ/jiLpw19YBiWw==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

Told through a rippling overlay of voices, Act of the Damned circles closer and closer to the revelation of the diabolical immorality of Diogo\'s greedy son-in-law Rodrigo . . . who has fathered a child of his own bastard daughter and who is closing in on Diogo\'s crumbling estate. <div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer13905447860277390521">Jos√© Saramago is one of the most important international writers of the last hundred years. Born in Portugal in 1922, he was in his sixties when he came to prominence as a writer with the publication of Baltasar and Blimunda. A huge body of work followed, translated into more than forty languages, and in 1998 he was awarded the Nobel Prize in Literature. Saramago died in June 2010.</span>

      <div class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/work/quotes/2338253-o-evangelho-segundo-jesus-cristo">Quotes from The Gospel Accord...</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
